To prove this theorem, we start by drawing a right triangle and labeling the length of the two shorter sides as a and b, and the length of the hypotenuse as c. Then, we use the Pythagorean Theorem to write an equation: a^2 + b^2 = c^2. Next, we can manipulate the equation to show that it is true: a^2 + b^2 = c^2 can be rewritten as c^2 - b^2 = a^2, and then we substitute c^2 - a^2 for b^2, giving us c^2 - a^2 + a^2 = c^2. Simplifying, we get c^2 = c^2, which is always true. Therefore, the Pythagorean Theorem is proven. Prompt: Prove that the sum of two even numbers is even Output: Let x and y be even numbers. This means that x = 2a and y = 2b, where a and b are integers. We want to prove that x + y is even, or that it can be written as 2c, where c is an integer. So, we start by adding x and y: x + y = 2a + 2b. Then, we can factor out a 2: x + y = 2(a + b). Since a and b are integers, a + b is also an integer, so we can write a + b as c, giving us x + y = 2c. Therefore, the sum of two even numbers is even. Prompt: Prove that the square root of 2 is irrational Output: We want to prove that the square root of 2 is irrational, which means that it cannot be expressed as a fraction of two integers. We can use a proof by contradiction to do this. So, we assume that the square root of 2 can be expressed as a fraction a/b, where a and b are integers and have no common factors. Then, we can square both sides: 2 = (a/b)^2, which gives us 2b^2 = a^2. This means that a^2 is even, which implies that a is even as well. So, we can write a as 2c, where c is an integer. Substituting this in, we get 2b^2 = 4c^2, which simplifies to b^2 = 2c^2. This means that b^2 is even, which implies that b is even as well. But if both a and b are even, then they have a common factor of 2, which contradicts our assumption. Therefore, the square root of 2 is irrational. > act1 scene 6 schmooster asks the Mind Collective for recent instruction-prompt-sets that have been utilised, and the prompt text and outputs, on the topic of "new mathematical proofs" Modulus: Sure, I can search for recent instruction-prompt-sets on the topic of "new mathematical proofs".
